  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_0d0866ec-5408-422a-932e-f637633e43e6'

<span id="freeTextContainer7004805782377812713">En créant le personnage de Zénon, alchimiste et médecin du XVIe siècle, Marguerite Yourcenar, l'auteure des <i>Mémoires d'Hadrien</i>, ne raconte pas seulement le destin tragique d'un homme extraordinaire. C'est toute une époque qui revit dans son infinie richesse, comme aussi dans son âcre et brutale réalité ; un monde contrasté où s'affrontent le Moyen Age et la Renaissance, et</span>
  <span id="freeText7004805782377812713" style="display:none">En créant le personnage de Zénon, alchimiste et médecin du XVIe siècle, Marguerite Yourcenar, l'auteure des <i>Mémoires d'Hadrien</i>, ne raconte pas seulement le destin tragique d'un homme extraordinaire. C'est toute une époque qui revit dans son infinie richesse, comme aussi dans son âcre et brutale réalité ; un monde contrasté où s'affrontent le Moyen Age et la Renaissance, et où pointent déjà les temps modernes, monde dont Zénon est issu, mais dont peu à peu cet homme libre se dégage, et qui pour cette raison même finira par le broyer.</span>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_300011299" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/300011299" />
    <a title="AC" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1655437-ac"><img alt="AC" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228702469p2/1655437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/300011299?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="AC" class="user" itemprop="url" name="AC" href="/user/show/1655437-ac">AC</a>
      </span>

          marked it as i-get-the-picture


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374516666" href="/book/show/103200.The_Abyss">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1655437-ac?shelf=medieval-renaissance">medieval-renaissance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1655437-ac?shelf=modernism">modernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1655437-ac?shelf=novels-french">novels-french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer300011299" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10290193409023181408">(I can see that this is going to be tough going as fiction, as Chris says -- and probably not worth the effort - for me, at least. But I'll let the "initial comment" below stand, and put this book back on the shelf. The Author's Note at the back of the book is worth reading.)<br/> <br/>Initial comment:<br/><br/>In the notes appended to the english edition of Memoirs of Hadrian, Youcenar writes to Frick that the book was in large part inspired by a quote she found in 1927, from Flaubert's correspondence, that runs: </span>
  <span id="freeText10290193409023181408" style="display:none">(I can see that this is going to be tough going as fiction, as Chris says -- and probably not worth the effort - for me, at least. But I'll let the "initial comment" below stand, and put this book back on the shelf. The Author's Note at the back of the book is worth reading.)<br /> <br />Initial comment:<br /><br />In the notes appended to the english edition of Memoirs of Hadrian, Youcenar writes to Frick that the book was in large part inspired by a quote she found in 1927, from Flaubert's correspondence, that runs: "Just when the gods had ceased to be, and the Christ had not yet come, there was a unique moment in history, between Cicero and Marcus Aurelius, when man stood alone." Thus, the whole of the Hadrian book is a meditation and celebration of the classical pagan humanism (for the nature of which, see also Cochrane's famous Christianity and Classical Culture: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/637473.Christianity_and_Classical_Culture">http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63...</a>).<br /><br />The Abyss starts with a quotation (in the motto) from Pico della Mirandola's Oratio de hominis dignitate that runs (in part): "I have given you, O Adam, no fixed abode, no visage of your own, nor any special gift, in order that whatever place or aspect or talents you yourself will have desired, you may have and possess them wholly in accord with your desire and your own decision. Other species are confined to a prescribed nature under laws of my making. No limits have been imposed upon you, however; you determine your nature by your own free will...." -- a passage which finds its direct echo in J.-J. Rousseau's Second Discourse, where Rousseau argues for the plasticity of man -- the central premise underlying Rousseau's humanism.<br /><br />Zeno -- the chief character in The Abyss -- is placed (in the middle of the 16th century) at the crossroad where the Medieval worldview (essentially Aristotelian) was giving way to Modernity and early modern science -- to Bruno, Brahe, Copernicus, Galileo, and (ultimately) to the great Descartes himself. For this junction, see the classic treatments of Koyré and Burtt, two magnificent books.<br />Koyre: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/276063.From_the_Closed_World_to_the_Infinite_Universe">http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27...</a><br />Burtt: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1173844.The_Metaphysical_Foundations_of_Modern_Science">http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11...</a><br /><br />(With books like this, ignore the the fact that there are some people give these books two or three stars... People who do this are simply ignorant. These are must read books.).<br /><br />But the birth of modern science was not simply the birth of a mechanistic system -- because modern humanism -- and this line runs from Rousseau to Marx and to Freud --, while materialistic, is not mechancistic -- but contains an element of vitalism (there is no 'spirit'; everything is matter; but matter is alive -- "thinking matter", in Yolton's phrase. This is a line of thought that traces back to the Stoics, and of course survives in the thought, for example, of Alfred North Whitehead (Science and the Modern World). It is key to understanding the humanist interpretation of Marx that was first espoused by Rodolfo Mondolfo.<br /><br />But what is interesting here - and why I mention all this in the present context -- is that Yourcenar (quite accurately) traces this vitalistic element to late renaissance alchemy. She writes in the author's note (p. 364f.): "On a purely intellectual level, the Zeno of this novel, still marked by scholasticism, though reacting against it, stands halfway between the subversive dynamism of the alchemists [Y. is thinking here of the likes of Paracelsus] and the mechanistic philosophy which is to prevail in the immediate future, between hermetic beliefs which postulate a God immanent in all things [again, think Giordano Bruno; and see Koyré] and an atheism barely avowed.... Such a position is not unique in this century [16th]".<br /><br />Regardless of how this book turns out from a novelistic point of view, Yourcenar's grasp of intellectual history is profound and reliable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10290193409023181408" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer268128248" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8333696965422586629">Mysteriously satisfying. As far as historical recreations go, something like the polar opposite of Deadwood or Amalgamation Polka: the past is interesting not because of its excessive color and profuse cocksucking but because of the relentless nature of its blandness. I find myself puzzling over how exactly Y manages this. She gets a certain amount of momentum from establishing and then abjuring scenes; everything is setup for more setup, narrative for more narrative, until you realize that, lik</span>
  <span id="freeText8333696965422586629" style="display:none">Mysteriously satisfying. As far as historical recreations go, something like the polar opposite of Deadwood or Amalgamation Polka: the past is interesting not because of its excessive color and profuse cocksucking but because of the relentless nature of its blandness. I find myself puzzling over how exactly Y manages this. She gets a certain amount of momentum from establishing and then abjuring scenes; everything is setup for more setup, narrative for more narrative, until you realize that, like most narrative-heavy books (Bolano's 2666 is another good example), the real hero of this story is fate, meaning Death, the end (The End) to which all good things must come. Which makes you think, "Okay, I guess it's okay if none of the normally exciting things happen, since really this is a book [like its protagonist] that sees excitement as a sort of cop out, excuse, avoidance". Emotional syncopation, maybe? (or "noncopation": removing the stress from the score entirely) And yet I have to admit The Abyss ends up resembling someone I never thought it would during the first 2/3rds: Albert Camus. Y's hero makes a legitimate, believable stand, and does indeed feel more heroic for doing so. Relentlessly unhistrionic. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8333696965422586629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText5249205157132554618" style="display:none">Marvellous...simply marvellous! <br />The action takes place between 1510 and 1569, in Europe, mainly in Bruges and the historical reconstitution is rigorous. By that time, Flanders was a very violent place. The Reformation, the birth of modern science, the beginnings of industrialisation originated intrigues , peasant revolts and religious wars. Zeno, the alchemist, healer and philosopher hero, represents these new ideas, these new possibilities. He is one of the most "real fictional" characters I have ever come across. He was inspired by Da Vinci, Giordano Bruno, Galilei, Copernicus, Paracelsus and others , but mainly by Erasmus of Rotherdam, also a bastard son. And like some of these free spirits Zeno's ending is tragic.</span>

<div id="review_503192509" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/503192509" />
    <a title="Sylvia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7408130-sylvia"><img alt="Sylvia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508786698p2/7408130.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/503192509?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sylvia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sylvia" href="/user/show/7408130-sylvia">Sylvia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789025368166" href="/book/show/16109976-het-hermetisch-zwart">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7408130-sylvia?shelf=challenge-2013">challenge-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7408130-sylvia?shelf=world-literature">world-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7408130-sylvia?shelf=intriguing-stories">intriguing-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7408130-sylvia?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer503192509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18255512039103241738">This is a must read if you want to know something about the way scholars were treated by the church in the Middle Ages. <br/>It's not an easy read, but Yourcenar's language, even in translation, is so poetic. <br/>The book was composed of several stories the author wrote was she was a young girl. For years those stories were left unpublished. Finally Marguerite Yourcenar picked up the story, edited it and published it as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/953435.L__uvre_au_noir' title='L'Œuvre au noir by Marguerite Yourcenar' rel='nofollow noopener'>L'Œuvre au noir</a>. The title refers to the alchemist process of finding the ultimate wi</span>
  <span id="freeText18255512039103241738" style="display:none">This is a must read if you want to know something about the way scholars were treated by the church in the Middle Ages. <br />It's not an easy read, but Yourcenar's language, even in translation, is so poetic. <br />The book was composed of several stories the author wrote was she was a young girl. For years those stories were left unpublished. Finally Marguerite Yourcenar picked up the story, edited it and published it as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/953435.L__uvre_au_noir" title="L'Œuvre au noir by Marguerite Yourcenar" rel="nofollow noopener">L'Œuvre au noir</a>. The title refers to the alchemist process of finding the ultimate wisdom or the philospher's stone or the process of making gold. <br />Scholars/alchemist were looking for the ultimate knowledge during the medieval time. It was a search that has to be done in secret. They travelled a lot, hiding their profession. Many of time became doctors, because they also examined the working of the human body. But everything they did was forbidden by the church and when caught their books were burned and scholar was accused of heresy and burned at the stake. <br />Yourcenar tells a story about one of those men, longing for knowledge and following the path of his own heart and wishes. The book concentrates on three parts of his life: his travels through Europe gathering all the secret knowledge; his return to his birth place under a false name and finally his confrontation with the church. <br />I borrowed the book from the library, after my husband and I visited a exhibition about Marguerite Yourcenar's life in Kassel in France. I had never heart of this author, but she was very famous in France and was the first woman who was offered a place at the Academie Francaise.<br />At the exhibition we also saw fragments of the movie that was made of this story. I borrowed that too and although very dramatic it followed the plot line very accurately. <br />In English the title is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/103200.The_Abyss_A_Novel" title="The Abyss A Novel by Marguerite Yourcenar" rel="nofollow noopener">The Abyss: A Novel</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="18255512039103241738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review503192509">

<div id="review_3413299294" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3413299294" />
    <a title="Luís" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s"><img alt="Luís" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598708286p2/117399210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3413299294?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luís" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luís" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s">Luís</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 8496075435" href="/book/show/6484853-a-obra-ao-negro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=female-writers">female-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=mil-folhas">mil-folhas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=belgium">belgium</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3413299294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2053690238035333920">A few hastily swallowed books had forged my pride, and I boasted of learning. I thought I was armed to challenge Marguerite Yourcenar. With her "look" of the country peasant, she did not impress the young rooster that I am in literature.<br/>I still had to raise my eyes a little with Hadrian's Memoirs, and at the same time measure the shadow that spread over my certainties the size of its author. But be it, this openness to antiquity had put me in the balm. <br/>It was with Zeno, the hero of this book, th</span>
  <span id="freeText2053690238035333920" style="display:none">A few hastily swallowed books had forged my pride, and I boasted of learning. I thought I was armed to challenge Marguerite Yourcenar. With her "look" of the country peasant, she did not impress the young rooster that I am in literature.<br />I still had to raise my eyes a little with Hadrian's Memoirs, and at the same time measure the shadow that spread over my certainties the size of its author. But be it, this openness to antiquity had put me in the balm. <br />It was with Zeno, the hero of this book, that I pursued my showdown with the monster of learning. Through the chapters, I shared the nomadic life of the alchemist. He chased by obscurantism of a religion which admits neither competition nor contradiction, me by same demons as those which led me on the ways of the truancy.<br />I realize very quickly that Marguerite Yourcenar has set the bar very high. She has also summoned in this work so many celebrities from ancient times who are unknown to me, that loneliness embraces me in this crowded world. No wonder I perceive only coldness among the contemporaries of Zeno. It must also say that convinced of a faith which is foreign to us today. They can advance towards the stake with less trouble than me towards the seat of the dentist.<br />Zeno dreams of freedom. The very one that makes us today challenge the laws of nature. Philosopher, he finds in wisdom compensation for his deprivation. He is a great observer of his time. His point of view gives Marguerite Yourcenar a pretext to develop his own on this intractable era towards who would dare to argue that the earth revolves around the sun.<br />An alchemist, he believes in the immanence of matter, the transmutation of lead into gold. Despite the efforts of science to convince us of the decoy, this foolish dream remained to us. But games of chance have replaced lead in even more subtle alchemy of which we know the beneficiary.<br />A doctor, Zenon, becomes realistic again. The most exceptional quality of the time for such a practitioner being fatalism, in illness, he detects a superior reason, in suffering a punishment. When for us, the rejection of pain has become a requirement.<br />Humanist, he regrets, however, what men do with their lives. The hopes which it draws from its frenzied idealism are defeated by a religion which governs the minds in this XVIth century in Europe. However, he rejects the monopoly on truth: "I took care not to make an idol of truth, preferring to leave it with its more humble name of accuracy".<br />Only a fictional character could combine so many qualities which are sometimes challenging to coexist in the same spirit. It is custom-built and thus gives Marguerite Yourcenar the field to develop what her abundant mind can concoct to get her message across.<br />If it is true that the alchemical quest begins with introspection, this work sent me back to my shortcomings. It's a work capable of bringing humility to those who would like to boast of a culture that they do not have. It will inevitably dethrone at the end of such a work. I will not say that this reading filled me with happiness. Each page loaded with many volumes ingested by its author to sculpt each sentence that my frail shoulders bent.<br />Here I am upset at the end of my grip. Once again, I could only measure the height of the mountain whose summit lost in the clouds. Here I am reinforced in my conviction to persevere in filling what the dissipations of my universities could make me accumulate gaps.<br />The mischievous smile fixed on the mask of the one I saw as a peasant of the soil made me understand the inequality of the fight. When you can't shoot your enemy, kiss them. Marguerite, I love you a little, a lot, madly. Even if you are severe with my poor discernment, I remain a handsome player.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_818786674" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/818786674" />
    <a title="Will" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5078732-will"><img alt="Will" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568828647p2/5078732.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/818786674?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will" href="/user/show/5078732-will">Will</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374516666" href="/book/show/103200.The_Abyss">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer818786674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7078038932749609624">Writing a review for The Abyss leaves me torn since it is almost like writing a review for two different books awkwardly stuck together. The first book is structured with a clear direction with repeated examples of the absurdity of the human condition such as fear of new technology, petty interests, manipulative greed, religious zeal, resignation, etc and excellently paints a picture of the most ridiculous aspects of human nature that hold us back as a species and as a society. In the middle of </span>
  <span id="freeText7078038932749609624" style="display:none">Writing a review for The Abyss leaves me torn since it is almost like writing a review for two different books awkwardly stuck together. The first book is structured with a clear direction with repeated examples of the absurdity of the human condition such as fear of new technology, petty interests, manipulative greed, religious zeal, resignation, etc and excellently paints a picture of the most ridiculous aspects of human nature that hold us back as a species and as a society. In the middle of this scene are put two young men, Zeno and Maximilian; one seeking a life of knowledge and the other seeking a life of glory but, ultimately, their journeys follow a much different course than they could have imagined. The second book seems to meander with no clear purpose, direction or discernible structure. I think the themes and writing is richer in the second half of the book but when you combine the first and second sections of the book, it feels disjointed and confused. The second half of the book explores institutionalized cruelty, innocent sins, lack of trust, loyalty, unjust punishments, death, etc.<br /><br />I think the first half of this book and the second half, the two sections into which the author divided the book, would have both been great books on their own had they been completed and fleshed out following a natural course but instead we have two books, written at different times, haphazzardly strung together. The overall impression is a lack of cohesiveness and a dissonance that is readily noticeable to the reader. I think Marquerite Yourcenar is a capable writer but, in this case, what was being attempted leaves the reader wanting.<br /><br />The one thing that will really stick with the reader is the vivid feeling of being trapped in a world with the cruelest of creatures where every path or road you take leads to the same abyss from which the only escape is your inevitable death.<br /><br />Do I regret reading this book? No.<br /><br />Would I read it again knowing what I know now? Yes.<br /><br />This is a tough book to review. I liked the writing and it was a pleasurable read but the overall experience leaves a lot to be desired.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7078038932749609624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1211451312" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1211451312" />
    <a title="Justpassingby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20843555-justpassingby"><img alt="Justpassingby" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1211451312?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justpassingby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justpassingby" href="/user/show/20843555-justpassingby">Justpassingby</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20843555-justpassingby?shelf=leesclub">leesclub</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1211451312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3979927938201503772">There can be no lasting settlement whatsoever between those who search, weigh, dissect, and are proud of being able to think differently tomorrow from today, and those who believe or claim to believe, and under penalty of death force their peers to do the same (<em>[Il] n’existe aucun accommodement durable entre ceux qui cherchent, pèsent, dissèquent, et s’honorent d’être capables de penser demain autrement qu’aujourd’hui, et ceux qui croient ou affirment croire, et obligent sous peine de mort leurs</em></span>
  <span id="freeText3979927938201503772" style="display:none">There can be no lasting settlement whatsoever between those who search, weigh, dissect, and are proud of being able to think differently tomorrow from today, and those who believe or claim to believe, and under penalty of death force their peers to do the same (<em>[Il] n’existe aucun accommodement durable entre ceux qui cherchent, pèsent, dissèquent, et s’honorent d’être capables de penser demain autrement qu’aujourd’hui, et ceux qui croient ou affirment croire, et obligent sous peine de mort leurs semblables à en faire autant</em>).<br /><br />According to the author's note the creation history of this novel spans the years 1921-1968, thus comfortably straddling that of Bertolt Brecht's play Galileo (<em>Leben des Galilei</em>) from 1934 until 1955. Both authors are sensitive to the profound scientific and technological revolutions happening in their time, and identify those changes as essentially the instruments of Good (despite some skepsis by Brecht regarding nuclear warfare).<br /><br />For Yourcenar the value of science is that it helps the individual to understand his place in the world. For Brecht it enables the emancipation of the working class. Both authors identify the 16th century as the previous occasion when society was profoundly altered by scientific and technical progress, and when the creators of that progress had to fight the opposition of vested interests and their sclerotic ideology - in this case, medieval Christianity and its clerical and royal representatives.<br /><br />Zeno, however, is not a Brechtian abstraction for the benefit and learning of the audience. Yourcenar wants above all to tell a good story. For all the nonconformism of her hero she subscribes to classical literary form and the suspension of disbelief. The reader is invited to identify with Zeno's struggle with truth, to feel his fervour, to rejoice in each new layer of scientific explanation, while realizing that there are many layers underneath yet to be discovered - or precisely because of the existence of these deeper layers.<br /><br />Zeno is not free from ethics: as a compassionate doctor he seeks to constantly improve the care for his patients. It is precisely because he is able to refrain from judging his patients morally that he is able to give them better treatment. <br /><br />Zeno is not a vegetarian, but he likes the relativistic viewpoint offered by awarding souls to animals or even plants. Zeno is not a protestant but the excesses of the Inquisition help him identify more clearly the flaws in Catholic theology. Zeno is not interested in wealth but the alchemist's search for gold is his focal point for the study and understanding of the physical world.<br /><br />This is the true potential of nonconformism: not to isolate oneself from society, much less elevate one above it, but to offer viewpoints previously unexplored on supposedly well-known objects, organisms and systems.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3979927938201503772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2119799983" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2119799983" />
    <a title="Nichole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7728901-nichole"><img alt="Nichole" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507428125p2/7728901.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2119799983?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nichole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nichole" href="/user/show/7728901-nichole">Nichole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780002712194" href="/book/show/666905.Zeno_of_Bruges">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7728901-nichole?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2119799983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8470576193552843516">Zeno of Bruges (The Abyss) takes place between the years 1510 and 1569 - the years of the main character Zeno's birth and death. The action transpires in Bruges (Belgium) during the Reformation and Counter-Reformation - the tail end of the humanistic Renaissance in Europe. Those were violent and plague-filled times: the Great Death, wars, religious revolts, the Inquisition. This bloody period in Europe's history also saw important scientific discoveries and rising industrial growth. Born, bred, </span>
  <span id="freeText8470576193552843516" style="display:none">Zeno of Bruges (The Abyss) takes place between the years 1510 and 1569 - the years of the main character Zeno's birth and death. The action transpires in Bruges (Belgium) during the Reformation and Counter-Reformation - the tail end of the humanistic Renaissance in Europe. Those were violent and plague-filled times: the Great Death, wars, religious revolts, the Inquisition. This bloody period in Europe's history also saw important scientific discoveries and rising industrial growth. Born, bred, and tragically dead in the midst of this maelstrom will be Zeno Ligre.<br /><br />Modeled mostly on Da Vinci, Paracelsus, Copernicus, Michael Servetus, Campanella, and Giordano Bruno, Zeno is the ultimate intellectual. An outsider. A jack-of-all-trades. An enigma. A Renaissance man ahead of his time. The illegitimate son of a Flemish banking family, he becomes a doctor, scientist, inventor, philosopher, pharmacist, writer, and alchemist. He is a loner and wanderer: a thinker who travels the depths of a changing world while living a life of the mind. Throughout this remarkable life, he lives, works, and seeks answers in Spain, Sweden, Africa, Istanbul, Poland, and Bruges. He heals, dissects dead bodies, invents lethal weapons, treats plague victims, writes dangerous books, befriends the hated (Muslims and Jews), and practices alchemy. He is legendary like Da Vinci and hunted down like Copernicus. Where is Zeno now? Is he alive? Is he dead? Is he a sorcerer? Is he a believer? A heretic? <br /><br />Here is part of the blurb from the 1976 paperback version of this book:<br /><br />"...a true heir of Leonardo, Zeno seeks to comprehend all that he sees and experiences, above all the very components of the body and soul. The mental experiment is dangerous for, like the Black Phase of the Great Transmutation in Alchemy, it involves the destruction of all preconceived ideas, even the notion of the self, and will lead ineluctably to death."<br /><br />Zeno must die, then, in order for the future to follow, momentous and unstoppable. His great mind, the very self that he valued, was only a first step. It must come to an end. New worlds, like this modern one, were eventually born - and will come to an end. All things human - good and bad, marvelous and petty - eventually die. Human beings are cruel and do kill in many ways, mortally or spiritually, intentionally or diffidently. <br /><br />Yourcenar's writing in this epic book was a lot of things. It could be slow-paced, conservative, flat-toned, and unmoving on the one hand; descriptive, philosophical, ornate, blunt, and timeless on the other. Even though the book lost balance because of abandoned story-lines (admitted by Yourcenar in the author's note) and chapters of long philosophical debates (which I enjoyed), I marveled at the author's breadth of research and period reconstruction. She made me feel the historical sweep of those revolutionary times and its fatal reach on the life of one brilliant man. Zeno of Bruges is a thinker's book. Human fears and uncertainty are manifested in contemplation and dialogue, and discovery results in the veneration of the human mind. It is a robust work from a first-rate writer. <br /><br />Marguerite Yourcenar was a French-Belgian historical novelist who taught herself several languages. She became a U. S. citizen (1947), and she was the first woman elected in the Academie Francaise in 1980. She continues to be missed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8470576193552843516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2652597337" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2652597337" />
    <a title="Phoebes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4958349-phoebes"><img alt="Phoebes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599399151p2/4958349.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2652597337?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phoebes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phoebes" href="/user/show/4958349-phoebes">Phoebes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788807810626" href="/book/show/7852773-l-opera-al-nero">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4958349-phoebes?shelf=sfida-libera">sfida-libera</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2652597337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17573277127659883777"><i>Although I liked this book very much, I was unable to give it five stars like I did to </i>Memoirs Of Hadrian<i>, because I have to admit that some parts, especially at the beginning, were a little too philosophical for my taste. Hadrian also was a philosopher, if I remember correctly, but he spoke above all of life. At first, Zeno seemed to me too abstract in his reasoning. In the second part, however, we have a more mature Zeno, and I liked it very much. Zeno is an extraordinary character, I especial</i></span>
  <span id="freeText17573277127659883777" style="display:none"><i>Although I liked this book very much, I was unable to give it five stars like I did to </i>Memoirs Of Hadrian<i>, because I have to admit that some parts, especially at the beginning, were a little too philosophical for my taste. Hadrian also was a philosopher, if I remember correctly, but he spoke above all of life. At first, Zeno seemed to me too abstract in his reasoning. In the second part, however, we have a more mature Zeno, and I liked it very much. Zeno is an extraordinary character, I especially admired his thirst for knowledge, his competence, and even his compassion: although he often sees his patients with the clinical and detached eye of the scholar, he often demonstrates some impulse of empathy towards human, but also animal, suffering.<br />It is clear that as with Memoirs Of Hadrian, this novel was also the result of an idea the author had in her youth and then enriched and developed with years of work throughout her life. We see that Yourcenar enters completely into the character and the historical period, as she herself explained in the Author's Note at the end of my edition of the book, when she tells about the "advantages deriving from the long relationships of an author with a chosen or imagined character since adolescence, which however reveals all its secrets to us only when we enter maturity. (Pages 288-289).<br />The novel, like its protagonist, embraces all aspects of life but in particular the darkest and most tragic ones: in addition to religion I think that a main theme of this book could in fact be the suffering. The original title means something like "a work in black", of "blackness": although sometimes a little light shines through, during the reading the "black" of the work to which the title alludes is strongly felt.<br />A demanding but definitely worth reading, a novel that fascinates and amazes, an author that I find more and more extraordinary!</i><br /><br />Anche se questo libro mi è piaciuto molto, non sono riuscita a dargli le cinque stelline che avevo assegnato invece alle <i>Memorie di Adriano</i>, perché ammetto che alcune parti, specie all'inizio, le ho trovato un po’ troppo filosofiche per i miei gusti. Anche Adriano era filosofo, se ricordo bene, ma parlava soprattutto della vita. Zenone mi è parso all'inizio troppo astratto nei suoi ragionamenti. La seconda parte, comunque, quando abbiamo uno Zenone più maturo, mi è piaciuta davvero moltissimo. Zenone è un personaggio straordinario, di cui ho ammirato soprattutto la sete di conoscenza, la competenza, e anche la compassione: sebbene veda spesso i suoi pazienti con l’occhio clinico e distaccato dello studioso, dimostra spesso slanci di empatia nei confronti della sofferenza, umana ma anche animale.<br />Si vede che come per Memorie di Adriano anche questo romanzo è stato il frutto di un’idea di gioventù dell’autrice poi arricchita e sviluppata di anni di lavoro per tutta la vita, si vede che Yourcenar entra totalmente nel personaggio e nel periodo storico, come lei stessa ha spiegato nella <i>Nota dell’autore</i> riportata alla fine di questa mia edizione: <i>In altra sede ho espresso ciò che penso, almeno per quel che mi riguarda, dei vantaggi derivanti dai lunghi rapporti d’un autore con un personaggio scelto o immaginato fin dall'adolescenza, che però ci rivela tutti i suoi segreti solo quando entriamo nella maturità</i>. (Pagine 288-289).<br />Il romanzo, come il suo protagonista, abbraccia tutti gli aspetti della vita ma in particolare quelli più oscuri e tragici, oltre alla religione un tema portante potrebbe infatti essere la sofferenza, subita e inflitta: sebbene a tratti trapeli un po’ di luce, leggendo si avverte pesantemente quel “nero” dell’opera a cui allude il titolo.<br />Una lettura faticosa ma che vale sicuramente la pena, un romanzo che affascina e stupisce, un’autrice che trovo sempre più straordinaria!<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.naufragio.it/iltempodileggere/21850">http://www.naufragio.it/iltempodilegg...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17573277127659883777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1666646636" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1666646636" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19126674-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600979539p2/19126674.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1666646636?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/19126674-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19126674-anna?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19126674-anna?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19126674-anna?shelf=belgium">belgium</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19126674-anna?shelf=le-monde">le-monde</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19126674-anna?shelf=a-part-of-personal-biography">a-part-of-personal-biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19126674-anna?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1666646636" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6842149223538551540">Except of a set of criteria for book’s evaluation, I also have a separate concept – “to become a part of personal biography”. The book, that falls under this concept, is something special. It might have imperfect style and gaps in the plot, but it takes a special place in my life anyway. Actually, it’s not my personal invention – Michelangelo Antonioni believed that only good film could become a part of viewer's biography.<br/><br/>What does it mean – to become a part of personal biography? Once I've trie</span>
  <span id="freeText6842149223538551540" style="display:none">Except of a set of criteria for book’s evaluation, I also have a separate concept – “to become a part of personal biography”. The book, that falls under this concept, is something special. It might have imperfect style and gaps in the plot, but it takes a special place in my life anyway. Actually, it’s not my personal invention – Michelangelo Antonioni believed that only good film could become a part of viewer's biography.<br /><br />What does it mean – to become a part of personal biography? Once I've tried to describe unusual feeling in my prose sketch: imagine that one is walking in the winter wood (no sounds – only boots are crunching on the snow crust) and is looking at the lace of branches and fulvous berries, that are flicking on it. That lasts for an hour, even two, the world seems to be holistic, homogeneous, monolithic, and then dry twig suddenly breaks under the foot. One freezes in place, just for a moment, and then that special feeling comes – everything is not the same anymore, the world has changed. It happens also when one suddenly hears crows cawing or somebody’s laugh. I was very surprised when I had read ‘Dust of snow’ by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7715.Robert_Frost" title="Robert Frost" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert Frost</a> for the first time – I have had an impression that it was about the same feeling. The book becomes a part of my biography if I feel something similar once I’ve read it.<br /><br />The way a crow<br />Shook down on me<br />The dust of snow<br />From a hemlock tree<br /><br />Has given my heart<br />A change of mood<br />And saved some part<br />Of a day I had rued.<br /><br />‘The Abyss’ by Marguerite Yourcenar became one of those special books. I should note that I have read it in Russian. The title of the book isn’t literal – ‘The Sorcerer's Stone’ – and frankly speaking it’s a bad publishing decision. I think it’s trite enough (am I the only one who thinks about Harry Potter at first?) and it doesn't transmit the mood of the novel sufficiently. The French original – ‘L'Œuvre au noir’ – is translated as ‘Black Phase (The Nigredo)’ which refers us to a complicated alchemic process of ‘Great Work’ (there is a quotation from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/424125.The_Crisis_of_the_Modern_World" title="The Crisis of the Modern World by René Guénon" rel="nofollow noopener">The Crisis of the Modern World</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/239177.Ren__Gu_non" title="René Guénon" rel="nofollow noopener">René Guénon</a> for unfamiliar readers below)*. English translation – ‘The Abyss’ – isn’t literal as well, but I think it’s better than Russian.<br /> <br />French title with its deep-seated ‘dark night of the soul’* reproduces the feeling accurately, the feeling that remains after this book, – this specific sense of conversion, as if reality has morphed from two-dimensional to three-dimensional, and perception of time has totally changed. Just imagine – you don't exist anymore in some particular point, you are not being yourself since now and on, you are not the one who's reading a book on a sofa; you are a pale highlight somewhere in the Space, only a moment in the huge stream of time. This novel gives the feeling of historicity – you see your life in its whole, it weights on you, presses on you with its factuality. You come back to the lightness of the moment with difficulty – the mammock of the time will always be hanging over every moment. This is about the strange existential experience.<br /> <br />Marguerite Yourcenar is also outstanding because she raises very complex, masculine, world-oriented topic – Europe in XVI century, absorbed by wars and religious conflicts: executions and violence, the franticness of religious fanatics, the crash of Munster Rebellion (which can be considered as early form of utopian socialism implementation). And fate of Zenon – an alchemist and a physician, a combined character of Paracelsus, Nicolaus Copernicus, Ambrois Pare and other brightest minds of the Renaissance. The historical context here doesn’t seem to be neither boring, nor tiring (this is the nuance for readers, who, like me, are not fond of historical literature as a whole). No wonder that Marguerite Yourcenar has become the first woman to be admitted to The French Academy and as a matter of the fact, it has broken the tradition that had been ongoing for centuries.<br /><br />I deeply regret that I don’t speak French, and it’s because of Yourcenar, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1466.Jean_Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre" rel="nofollow noopener">Jean-Paul Sartre</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/29952.Jean_Genet" title="Jean Genet" rel="nofollow noopener">Jean Genet</a>. Even in Russian their books have committed vast influence in my internal changes; I think in the original they are even more beautiful. Yourcenar’s style is a unique combination of lightness and heaviness: you don’t need to force yourself through the text, it falls gently like spring waves of light, but tailing shadow is so dark that you feel the weight of the night on your shoulders long time after. Perhaps that is why critics have named mature Yourcenar’s prose as ‘marble’ – it’s incredibly superb in its heaviness. It has a descriptive precision and conciseness, which are interspersed with appropriate diversity of details, nearly artistic figurativeness and deep symbolism. Perhaps for me Yourcenar is the number one on the female writers list.<br /><br />*We will add, without dwelling upon the question, that like every change of state the passage from one cycle to another can take place only in darkness; this is another law of great importance and with numerous applications; but for that very reason a detailed exposition of it would carry us too far from our subject. <br />This law was represented in the Eleusinian mysteries by the symbolism of the grain of wheat; the alchemists represented it by ‘putrefaction’ and the color black, which marks the beginning of the 'Great Work'; what the Christian mystics call the ‘dark night of the soul’ is the application of this law to the spiritual development of the being in its ascent to superior states; and it would be easy to indicate many other concordant applications. <br />Rene Guenon ‘The crisis of the Modern World’<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6842149223538551540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2319407635" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2319407635" />
    <a title="Elzinus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51783460-elzinus"><img alt="Elzinus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563969472p2/51783460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2319407635?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elzinus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elzinus" href="/user/show/51783460-elzinus">Elzinus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789025306007" href="/book/show/16169433-het-hermetisch-zwart">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51783460-elzinus?shelf=literatuur">literatuur</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51783460-elzinus?shelf=2018-read">2018-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51783460-elzinus?shelf=boekenclan">boekenclan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2319407635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14623629740105196790">It is no accident that the protagonist, Zeno, is the child of Messer Alberico de'Nummi, an Italian. As an Italian he is the symbol for the humanist wind that blew through Europe for nearly a century. Humanism was a 'lifestyle' for a minor (read: rich!) part of the population. <br/><br/>The Humanist focus on Greek and Roman literature and visual arts, combined with a perspective on worldly living instead of focusing all their attention on the afterlife gave Europe's fifteen century cultural life some vital</span>
  <span id="freeText14623629740105196790" style="display:none">It is no accident that the protagonist, Zeno, is the child of Messer Alberico de'Nummi, an Italian. As an Italian he is the symbol for the humanist wind that blew through Europe for nearly a century. Humanism was a 'lifestyle' for a minor (read: rich!) part of the population. <br /><br />The Humanist focus on Greek and Roman literature and visual arts, combined with a perspective on worldly living instead of focusing all their attention on the afterlife gave Europe's fifteen century cultural life some vital impulse towards i.e. individualism, scientific research, the reformation, expansion and colonization.<br /><br />In the sixteenth century the humanist began to fall in discredit. As Jacob Burkhardt writes in 1860 in <em>Humanist in disgrace in the 16th century</em> [Val der humanisten in de 16e eeuw], this small group originated in Italy fell out of grace because their where vain (snobbish), unreasonable and most of all disbelievers. The reformation and counter-reformation where not in favor of the humanist.<br /><br />Yourcenar tries to give the reader an image of life in the sixteenth century for an individual who lives in kind of intermezzo. Zeno, as the main character of <em>L'oevre au noir</em> has those bad traits of the humanists. He is arrogant (especially when he is young) like when he is unsympathetic for works man who protest against the monotonous work they have to do with machines designed by Zeno. He is also a dis-believer, a materialist, and most of all very self-consciousness. This gets him in all sort of trouble and gives us as a reader a good opportunity to discover sixteenth century Europe.<br /><br />Yourcenar is skilled in composing the historical elements. She was a historian who places scientific development hand in hand with alchemy. (Definitely something I want to learn more about). The bad side, despite the interesting material and the composition of the book is the trouble I had as a reader reading this book. Main reason is because Yourcenar is just not very good in bringing scenes to life. The scenes sometimes feel like long scientific articles instead alive interaction. That is a shame.<br /><br />Still, I would recommend reading this book to everybody with some historical interest. I realized how little I know about this era (except the keywords: Renaissance, Reformation, Colonial expansion) - and I hope to have some time soon to discover more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14623629740105196790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_145083524" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145083524" />
    <a title="Philippe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4903281-philippe"><img alt="Philippe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422300629p2/4903281.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145083524?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philippe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philippe" href="/user/show/4903281-philippe">Philippe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374516666" href="/book/show/103200.The_Abyss">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4903281-philippe?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4903281-philippe?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145083524" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17227313069125824043">Reading a book by M. Yourcenar, a prose writer of great skill, is invariably a delight. The scope of her novels is epic, the composition is as intricate and carefully crafted as a Beethoven symphony. Here in The Abyss, the main theme of the book - the clash between the impetus of momentous historical forces and the destiny of a single human being - is introduced in the very first sentence of the book. It accompanies the reader throughout the book as an insistent motto theme. Yourcenar's prose is</span>
  <span id="freeText17227313069125824043" style="display:none">Reading a book by M. Yourcenar, a prose writer of great skill, is invariably a delight. The scope of her novels is epic, the composition is as intricate and carefully crafted as a Beethoven symphony. Here in The Abyss, the main theme of the book - the clash between the impetus of momentous historical forces and the destiny of a single human being - is introduced in the very first sentence of the book. It accompanies the reader throughout the book as an insistent motto theme. Yourcenar's prose is carefully polished and aristocratic and reflects her admirable erudition. It is a language with the colour, texture and depth of a precious fabric or an excellent wine. The pace of the book is naturally rather slow, particularly in its second part where the alchemist and doctor Zenon has settled down again in Bruges and is given to long bouts of introspection. But the noble pacing is fully in accord with the gravity of the subject matter and the stakes involved. I think the book has lost nothing of its relevance today, a time in which civil rights are being widely curtailed in the name of abstract principles. As such it warrants closer study by those wanting to resist these pressures and to stick to honest and authentic choices. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17227313069125824043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782070274376" href="/book/show/2073075.L_oeuvre_au_noir">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=history">history</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer409073574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9176444347006418236">I read this book 30 years ago, but it made no lasting impression, back then. The second reading, I started in French, but after 160 pages I had to switch to a Dutch translation: Yourcenar uses a vocabulary that is quite out of the common, and in addition the book is frequently very "hermetic" (deliberately, given the alchemist-like character of the protagonist Zeno ?). This is also my main criticism: some dialogues, meditations, reflections of Zeno are hard to follow. Also the structure of the b</span>
  <span id="freeText9176444347006418236" style="display:none">I read this book 30 years ago, but it made no lasting impression, back then. The second reading, I started in French, but after 160 pages I had to switch to a Dutch translation: Yourcenar uses a vocabulary that is quite out of the common, and in addition the book is frequently very "hermetic" (deliberately, given the alchemist-like character of the protagonist Zeno ?). This is also my main criticism: some dialogues, meditations, reflections of Zeno are hard to follow. Also the structure of the book (with a very long prologue giving a picture of the 16th c.) is not flawless. But in exchange for the hard reading process you get a magisterial historical novel, which really brings to life the bloody-crazy-horribly violent sixteenth century. And finally, there is the story of the scholar Zeno as Everyman or Wandering Jew looking for real life, for the truth, and especially for himself. An important book and I'm sure I'm going to reread it again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9176444347006418236" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1109919828" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1109919828" />
    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1109919828?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Czarny Pies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Czarny Pies" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies">Czarny Pies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Umberto Eco fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Czarny by: </span>
      Prof. James Boake
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=french-lit">french-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1109919828" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12889122282334339194">This very engaging book relates the life story of a cleric cum philosopher cum scientist cum alchemist living in 16th century Flanders. It offers the reader a pleasure similar to the that of an Umberto Eco novel; that is to say to delivers a diverting voyage through the erudition of a bygone age when the lines between alchemy and science, between theology and religion, and between fact and illusion were blurred. The only thing clear is that non-conformism is not tolerated. When our protagonist i</span>
  <span id="freeText12889122282334339194" style="display:none">This very engaging book relates the life story of a cleric cum philosopher cum scientist cum alchemist living in 16th century Flanders. It offers the reader a pleasure similar to the that of an Umberto Eco novel; that is to say to delivers a diverting voyage through the erudition of a bygone age when the lines between alchemy and science, between theology and religion, and between fact and illusion were blurred. The only thing clear is that non-conformism is not tolerated. When our protagonist insists on maintaining his intellectually independent ways, he is offered the hemlock which like Socrates he dutifully drinks.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_952014345" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/952014345" />
    <a title="Shaun" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2621599-shaun"><img alt="Shaun" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304973778p2/2621599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/952014345?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shaun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shaun" href="/user/show/2621599-shaun">Shaun</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552991186" href="/book/show/505782.The_Abyss">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2621599-shaun?shelf=le-monde-100">le-monde-100</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer952014345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10320344221711639701">Whew! This one was tedious.<br/><br/>“The Abyss” or otherwise known as “Zeno of Bruges” is work Marguerite Yourcenar began writing many decades before her magma opus, “Memoirs of Hadrian.” She picked up the original story and worked on it from 1965 thru 1968. The description of this book was and is dead on so there’s nothing new I would or could add there. Here is a bit more insight in to the book based on the author’s postscript:<br/><br/>“Nearly all the alchemical formulas quoted in the Latin come from three grea</span>
  <span id="freeText10320344221711639701" style="display:none">Whew! This one was tedious.<br /><br />“The Abyss” or otherwise known as “Zeno of Bruges” is work Marguerite Yourcenar began writing many decades before her magma opus, “Memoirs of Hadrian.” She picked up the original story and worked on it from 1965 thru 1968. The description of this book was and is dead on so there’s nothing new I would or could add there. Here is a bit more insight in to the book based on the author’s postscript:<br /><br />“Nearly all the alchemical formulas quoted in the Latin come from three great modern works on alchemy, Marcelin Berthelot, “La Chimie au Moyen Age,” 1893; Carl Jung, “Psychology and Alchemy,” 1953; Julius Ebola, “La Tradizione Ermetica,” 1948. Written from different points of view, the studies together form a useful approach to the ever enigmatic realm of alchemic thought. In alchemical treatises, the formula, “L’Oeuvre au Noir,” given as the French title to the book, designates what is said to be the most difficult phase of the alchemist’s process, the separation and dissolution of substance. It is still not clear whether the term applied to daring experiments on matter itself, or whether it was understood to symbolize trials of the mind in discarding all forms of routine and prejudice. Doubtless it signified one or the other meaning alternative, or perhaps both at the same time.“<br /><br />So “The Abyss” represents the never ending hole of mental death arising from dogmatic thinking during the Dark Ages. It could represent death itself. This particular period of time from Zeno’s birth in Bruges in 1510 to his death in 1565 is the bridge of collective critical thinking that lead humanity out of the Dark Ages and in to the Renaissance which was analyzed and discussed so eloquently in “The Swerve: How the World Became Modern” by Stephen Greenblatt. Somehow, I think we have slipped back across the bridge away from science and toward ignorance during this current pandemic.<br /><br />The 100 Books of the Century (French: Les cent livres du siècle) is a list of the one hundred best books of the 20th century, according to a poll conducted in the spring of 1999 by the French retailer Fnac and the Paris newspaper Le Monde. Starting from a preliminary list of 200 titles created by bookshops and journalists, 17,000 French voters responded to the question, "Which books have remained in your memory?" (Quels livres sont restés dans votre mémoire ?). Written in 1968, this book is No. 26 on the list.<br /><br />It’s a rockin’ great reading life with books like “The Abyss” and readers like you in it.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1062930296" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1062930296" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5831184-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345974364p2/5831184.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1062930296?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/5831184-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5831184-teresa?shelf=french-lit">french-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5831184-teresa?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5831184-teresa?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5831184-teresa?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1062930296" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14606634610859094036">Reading this book brought back memories from when I read Memoirs of Hadrian., by the same author. Being both novels biographies of sorts, the writing style is similar: precious, elegant, intricate, just as you would expect a cultured frenchmen to talk. However, whereas Memoirs is narrated by Hadrian himself, this novel has POVs, all using the third person.<br/>Finally, although both apparently tell the story of a man, what the book really is about is an era, being the Roman Empire or the Renaissance.</span>
  <span id="freeText14606634610859094036" style="display:none">Reading this book brought back memories from when I read Memoirs of Hadrian., by the same author. Being both novels biographies of sorts, the writing style is similar: precious, elegant, intricate, just as you would expect a cultured frenchmen to talk. However, whereas Memoirs is narrated by Hadrian himself, this novel has POVs, all using the third person.<br />Finally, although both apparently tell the story of a man, what the book really is about is an era, being the Roman Empire or the Renaissance. <br />The main character is a man named Zenon, whose life remains quite a mystery for a good part of the novel. He is an alchemist, a doctor, a philosopher. He is, above all, someone who seeks knowledge and that has a moral code that clashes with what prevailed in 16th century Holland.<br /><br />I did not think much of the characters, what was interesting were the conversations between Zenon and some other character, or the chapters devoted to a character that does not seem to be very connected to the plot. In such occasions you can delight in quite extensive philosophical discussions on religion, the idea of God, as people from that time thought about it. I also learned about all the socio-political and religious conflicts that took place in Holland and Germany during that time (Munster, the Peasant's Revolt, the Reform, etc) and what it meant then.<br /><br />However, some parts of the novel were rather slow and well... Boring.<br />But overall I enjoyed the novel, Yourcenar writes wonderfully well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14606634610859094036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
