    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3272816520" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3272816520" />
    <a title="Alex Sarll" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5866774-alex-sarll"><img alt="Alex Sarll" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453246877p2/5866774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3272816520?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex Sarll" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex Sarll" href="/user/show/5866774-alex-sarll">Alex Sarll</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552991186" href="/book/show/505782.The_Abyss">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3272816520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18279804995895867231">"On returning home, that is to say, to prison, and knowing well that the outcome of this malady of incarceration would be fatal, Zeno tried to find ways to keep from reflecting."<br/><br/>For saying how much SFF and Wodehouse and superheroes I read, I can be really quite shit at escapism.<br/><br/>Yourcenar's novel of the tumultuous 16th century chronologically overlaps Hilary Mantel's Cromwell trilogy, whose final volume I also finished in lockdown, but for saying this is a much shorter book it operates on a much </span>
  <span id="freeText18279804995895867231" style="display:none">"On returning home, that is to say, to prison, and knowing well that the outcome of this malady of incarceration would be fatal, Zeno tried to find ways to keep from reflecting."<br /><br />For saying how much SFF and Wodehouse and superheroes I read, I can be really quite shit at escapism.<br /><br />Yourcenar's novel of the tumultuous 16th century chronologically overlaps Hilary Mantel's Cromwell trilogy, whose final volume I also finished in lockdown, but for saying this is a much shorter book it operates on a much broader canvas. Most obviously, England barely merits a thought here until pretty late on when the protagonist, Zeno, has more or less burned everywhere else he can run - and even then he ultimately doesn't bother. More than that, though, it exists in a wider intellectual world; where Cromwell, for all his slipperiness in other respects, always had his Protestant certainties, characters here vacillate between the greater and lesser poles of the period's religious fractures, or, in the case of Zeno himself, transcend them and regard the whole business with much the same pained bemusement as we moderns. Which is in some senses The Abyss' great weakness; while it was begun at the same time as Yourcenar's masterpiece, Memoirs Of The Emperor Hadrian, it doesn't ever manage quite that same sense of being a transmission direct from the era it described. In her concluding author's note, Yourcenar points out that while Zeno is her creation, he partakes of real contemporaries such as Leonardo and Erasmus, Giordano Bruno, Tycho Brahe. Which he does. And yes, even what seem the too prescient warnings about the destructive uses to which mankind would one day put what was not yet known as science are taken from alchemical texts of the time. But that's just it – these were all remarkable individuals, and to borrow from all of them (and others) makes Zeno that little bit too special, too wise in too many fields, his life too eventful and intersecting with too many other famous people and incidents.<br /><br />Not that this is Zeno's book so thoroughly as Cromwell's trilogy was his, or the Memoirs were Hadrian's. At first he doesn't even seem set to be the protagonist; we're introduced initially to his cousin, Henry Maximilian Ligre, and for a time their stories advance in tandem; the alchemist and the soldier, one preferring boys and the other girls (though neither being quite as determined in those preferences as later centuries might have expected). The reader might well expect the counterpoint to continue throughout, especially when the two men's paths occasionally cross in later life, leading to conversations which are more philosophical dialogue than family chat. But Zeno long outlasts his kinsman, simply because both of them live perilous lives in uncertain times, and one of their lucks runs out before the other's. Nor is Henry Maximilian the only other character whose head we get inside; even the motives of quite minor players are laid open to us from an omniscient perspective which often knows them far better than they know themselves. The trade-off being that we never feel we are them in quite the same way as we could Cromwell or Hadrian; at most we're watching alongside them, but distinct from them, within their head. But this feels somehow more appropriate when one considers the art of the period and the area - the author's note mentions Bosch and Brueghel, and explains that a fragment of an earlier version of The Abyss appeared under a title suggesting, a little misleadingly, that it was an exercise in a prose rendition of Dürer. With all of those too, I see wonderfully rendered exteriors without fully feeling I know the subjects - and the degree to which each of us must in the end go through life alone is a big theme here. <br /><br />At the same time, of course, like most historical or future-set fiction (though somehow not Hadrian), it is also a novel of the times in which it arrived. Having lived through the World Wars, not quite making it to the end of the Cold War, you can see why Yourcenar would be fascinated with looking back at the fiery birth of the modern world. For all that she began the first version of The Abyss young, this iteration feels very much like what it was, the work of a great mind's latter days. Zeno surely speaks for his creator when he thinks of having seen so many people and even some ideas die, and then sometimes come again under new names; equally, ideas whose birth he remembers are now assumed to have been around since time immemorial. On the other hand, some things never change, not least the way that those with laudable aims of overturning injustice, once they have the whip hand, soon descend into their own horrific persecutions. There is always the temptation, and plenty of supporting evidence, to believe that the world is in its last days. And of course, now more than at its publication in the seventies, there is the plague. An historian mate of mine is being driven to distraction lately by her colleagues' hot takes comparing past plagues to COVID-19, but hey! I'm just an apophenic amateur so I can and will draw correspondences with abandon! Not that it's exactly hard work with material like "The physicians of the city, he said, were either worn out or were stricken themselves, or else were firmly decided not to contaminate their regular patients by visiting the plague victims".<br /><br />Correspondences, of course, also being key to alchemy, which runs through the book. I know just enough to be fairly sure the whole novel also recapitulates an allegorical alchemical narrative, without actually being able to point to the exact stages or draw any useful conclusion from it. It's most pronounced in the eponymous chapter, one of the two most vertiginous sections in a book that frequently operates in that mode. Here we follow Zeno on an inward odyssey, thinking about thinking; it's a profoundly destabilising and draining read despite the fluency of the prose (where, as in most of her books, Yourcenar worked on the English herself along with her lover Grace Frick).<br /><br />The other especially powerful chapter, of course, is the end. One can necessarily never be sure how well a writer has captured the experience of dying, but having seen the experience from the outside, this is certainly as evocative a description of what it feels like from inside as I think I'll ever need. Yet somehow, after everything, it's almost uplifting.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18279804995895867231" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1118510467" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1118510467" />
    <a title="Andrez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2751946-andrez"><img alt="Andrez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461452316p2/2751946.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1118510467?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrez" href="/user/show/2751946-andrez">Andrez</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789722037600" href="/book/show/13638194-a-obra-ao-negro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2751946-andrez?shelf=zzz-2015">zzz-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2751946-andrez?shelf=in-portuguese">in-portuguese</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1118510467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11098125558610940622">L'œuvre au noir (literally, oeuvre to black ) or, for English readers, The Abyss, is considered one of the two greatest works of Yourcenar, together with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12172.Memoirs_of_Hadrian' title='Memoirs of Hadrian by Marguerite Yourcenar' rel='nofollow noopener'>Memoirs of Hadrian</a>. There are some obvious similarities between the two works: their main characters are both free-thinking men, visionaries ahead of their time whose brilliance both makes them shine and detaches them from the rest of society; both are written somewhat introspectively, narrating the evolution of the main character's life; and b</span>
  <span id="freeText11098125558610940622" style="display:none">L'œuvre au noir (literally, oeuvre to black ) or, for English readers, The Abyss, is considered one of the two greatest works of Yourcenar, together with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12172.Memoirs_of_Hadrian" title="Memoirs of Hadrian by Marguerite Yourcenar" rel="nofollow noopener">Memoirs of Hadrian</a>. There are some obvious similarities between the two works: their main characters are both free-thinking men, visionaries ahead of their time whose brilliance both makes them shine and detaches them from the rest of society; both are written somewhat introspectively, narrating the evolution of the main character's life; and both have some very interesting themes. <br />Zeno is a man of great intelligence, a true Renaissance man, who leaves a safe life of priesthood to wander Europe and amass knowledge. He's a philosopher, a physician and an alchemist who ends up being chased by the Inquisition because of his novel ideas and different thinking. He's basically the paradigm of the Medieval scientist. The true essence of the book is his evolution, that mirrors the various phases needed to achieve the creation of the Philosopher's Stone: blackening, from which the work's name comes, whitening, yellowing and reddening, as well as his reflections on the world that surrounds him.<br /><br />While it didn't quite trap me like Memoirs of Hadrian, I can say this is a great book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11098125558610940622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/319372194" />

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789722022477" href="/book/show/2433706.A_Obra_ao_Negro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8220634-john?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8220634-john?shelf=favourite-reads">favourite-reads</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer319372194" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12531597657469978654">The first thing I ever read written about Marguerite Yourcenar was simply "<b>Yourcenar conjures worlds.</b>" It was with this revealing piece of information that I went into one of the better known works of an immensely powerful writer, bearer of an equally powerful name. And, try as I may, I cannot find the words to put it any better: Yourcenar <i>does</i> conjure worlds, as she taps into the depths of a complex, high symbolical story that finds its ideal way of storytelling in the heavy, description-based </span>
  <span id="freeText12531597657469978654" style="display:none">The first thing I ever read written about Marguerite Yourcenar was simply "<b>Yourcenar conjures worlds.</b>" It was with this revealing piece of information that I went into one of the better known works of an immensely powerful writer, bearer of an equally powerful name. And, try as I may, I cannot find the words to put it any better: Yourcenar <i>does</i> conjure worlds, as she taps into the depths of a complex, high symbolical story that finds its ideal way of storytelling in the heavy, description-based prose that engulfs us in its overwhelming pull of fiction and reality, melding the two together into one, and placing us there – <i>there </i>, where everything is taking place.<br /><br />I adored this book. It had been a long time since I had read something so simply (and yet complexly) gripping; raw and real, violent yet sweet; arcane in a manner – so subtly to the point that one can’t help but feel reverence for the mind and mastery of this alchemist of words, as she takes the human and mundane to transmute it into something important and divine. By the end, I cannot say if I read this book or if this book read me. <br /><br /><br />Nigredo? Rubedo, for sure.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12531597657469978654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText17152916838195128002" style="display:none"><br />-spoilers hereunder-<br /><br /><br />This is the story of Zen, a "belgian" surgeon/philosopher/alchemist in the Middle Ages - from his childhood through his european journeys and finally to his suicide - at the times of the black death and the inquisition. The book - written by Yourcenar, an author who has since then been called the last of the classic french writers - is heavy with mesmorising dialogues about the place of religion, and science in the world of men, in an era when raising such questions could lead you straight to your own demise.<br />Zeno is in some way announcing the end of the middle ages. While believing that men will lead to the end of Man, the novel goes through his inner struggles, those of the humanist trying to keep together what men destroy around him (including themselves), those of the young priest having turned away from the power-hungry man-killing church, those of the scientific mind confronted with its own disappointment about human nature...the story of a pessimistic mind with an optimistic heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17152916838195128002" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/1653081-becky">Becky</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40849715" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9561015549735279669">I'm sure this is more wonderful book for some folks that it was for me. I liked it well enough but beyond that ... well... I know I have the history background for it but it's the philosophical background I'm lacking. I wasn't too fond of the way Yourcenar invented her main characters by amalgamating them from historical characters and using "typical" situations for their adventures (some real ones). She was obviously more focused on the philosophy and the ideas of a renegade philosopher/alchemi</span>
  <span id="freeText9561015549735279669" style="display:none">I'm sure this is more wonderful book for some folks that it was for me. I liked it well enough but beyond that ... well... I know I have the history background for it but it's the philosophical background I'm lacking. I wasn't too fond of the way Yourcenar invented her main characters by amalgamating them from historical characters and using "typical" situations for their adventures (some real ones). She was obviously more focused on the philosophy and the ideas of a renegade philosopher/alchemist/renaissance man and his adventures in the bloody battles of the Reformation. Medieval medicine is also a focus - to show that our hero, Zeno, part Stoic part Buddhist, all renaissance man, is so far advanced. <br /><br />Overall I'm glad I read it because it helps me clarify what I appreciate in historical fiction. Sometimes Yourcenar does wonderfully - in her depiction of the Muenster rebellion for instance and the trial of Zeno. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9561015549735279669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HJTSZSmydzj+xTDbn5/eSDoRpSbXIoK+DlbYZ5U1rPPgizP/9nw6bjZK12pdOBis/VFB0UKVO3M9Shu1WiDJ0w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_28370'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28370.The_Blue_Flowers?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Blue Flowers<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/15957.Raymond_Queneau?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Raymond Queneau<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.04 avg rating &mdash; 2,170 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1965\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer5201811750678370129\">At his death in 1976, Raymond Queneau was one of France\'s most eminent men of letters – novelist, poet, essayist, editor, scientist, mathematician, and, more to the point, pataphysician. And only a pataphysician nurtured lovingly on surrealist excess<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5201811750678370129\" style=\"display:none\">At his death in 1976, Raymond Queneau was one of France\'s most eminent men of letters – novelist, poet, essayist, editor, scientist, mathematician, and, more to the point, pataphysician. And only a pataphysician nurtured lovingly on surrealist excess could have dreamed up The Blue Flowers, Queneau\'s 1964 novel, now reissued as a New Directions Paperbook. To a pataphysician all things are equal, there is no improvement or progress in the human condition, and a ‘message’ is an invention of the benighted reader, certainly not the author or his perplexing creations – the sweet, fennel-drinking Cidrolin, and the rampaging Duke d\'Auge. History is mostly what the duke rampages through – 700 years of it at 175-year clips. He refuses to crusade, clobbers his king with the ‘in’ toy of 1439 – the cannon – dabbles in alchemy, and decides that those musty caves down at Altamira need a bit of sprucing up. Meanwhile, Cidrolin in the 1960s lolls on his barge moored along the Seine, sips essence of fennel, and ineffectually tries to catch the graffitist who nightly defiles his fence. But mostly he naps. Is it just a coincidence that the duke appears only when Cidrolin is dozing? And vice versa? In the tradition of Villon and Céline, Queneau attempted to bring the language of the French streets into common literary usage, and his mad word-plays, bad puns, bawdy jokes, and anachronistic wackiness have been kept amazingly and glitteringly intact by the incomparable translator Barbara Wright.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"5201811750678370129\" href=\"#\" Eco <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16918381101907715051\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;Come stroll with me through the leafy glades of narrative...&quot; With Umberto Eco as companion and guide, who could resist such an invitation? In this exhilarating book, we accompany him as he explores the intricacies of fictional form and method. Eco draws us in by means of a novelist\'s techniques, making us his collaborators in the creation of his text and in the investigation of some of fiction\'s most basic mechanisms. How does a text signal the type of reader it wants, and how does it &quot;stage&quot; for us, through its style and voice, a certain version of the author? What is the relation between this &quot;model reader&quot; and &quot;model author&quot;? How does narrative lead us on, persuade us to lose ourselves in its depths? The range of Eco\'s examples is astonishing - from fairy tales, through Flaubert, Poe, and Manzoni, to Ian Fleming, Mickey Spillane, and Casablanca. In a detailed analysis of one of his favorite texts, Gerard de Nerval\'s Sylvie, Eco examines the uses of temporal ambiguity, demystifying the &quot;mists&quot; in the literary forest. In another chapter, he takes detective fiction and pornography as a basis for discussing narrative pace - strategic speeding up and slowing down - and the relationship between real time and narrative time. And in yet another chapter, we follow Eco as he shadows the musketeer D\'Artagnan through the streets of seventeenth-century Paris, a trail that leads us to the uncertain boundary between story and history. Fiction is parasitically dependent on reality; but reality, too, feeds on fiction. Here, the book reveals its serious side. What are the implications for society when the line between reality and fiction becomes blurred? How are stories (&quot;plots&quot; in the mostinsidious sense of the word) constructed over the course of time? In order to be responsible citizens of the world, Eco shows, we must be skilled and incisive readers. Getting lost in the blurry region where the real and the fictional merge can be a disturbing experience.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7732.Marguerite_Yourcenar">About Marguerite Yourcenar</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7732.Marguerite_Yourcenar"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1483207978p3/7732.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7732.Marguerite_Yourcenar">Marguerite Yourcenar
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
973
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9131381742228379370">The first woman to be elected to The French Academy, the multi talented personality of Marguerite Yourcenar was a novelist, essayist, playwright, short story writer, poet and translator. She was an artist at reconstructing historical eras in the form of her novels. Her novels, dealing with modern issues set in historical eras won her immense fame as a writer.<br/><br/>The inheritance Yourcenar received afte</span>
  <span id="freeText9131381742228379370" style="display:none">The first woman to be elected to The French Academy, the multi talented personality of Marguerite Yourcenar was a novelist, essayist, playwright, short story writer, poet and translator. She was an artist at reconstructing historical eras in the form of her novels. Her novels, dealing with modern issues set in historical eras won her immense fame as a writer.<br /><br />The inheritance Yourcenar received after the death of her father, allowed her to devote herself to traveling and literary pursuits and love affairs on the Paris artistic scene. She joined a bohemian crowd of artists, moving between Paris, Lausanne, Athens, Istanbul, Brussels and the Greek islands, which inspired her travel writing "Nouvelles Orientales" and "Feux" based on Greek mythology.<br /><br />Yourcenar was among the first female writers to live an openly lesbian lifestyle, and she moved to the USA in 1939 to live with her partner Grace Frick. The couple were to remain together until Frick’s death in 1979. She taught French History and History of Art, and continued to write: her novel "Mémoires d’Hadrien" was published in 1951 and enjoyed success with both the critics and the public worldwide. <br /><br />She was an inveterate traveller and remained active right up to her death in 1987. She was a militant vegetarian and defender of the rights of animals and inspired Brigitte Bardot to set up her animal sanctuary.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9131381742228379370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
